The star power in the building for this event, as always, was blinding. But at one point during the two-hour Class of 2019 enshrinement ceremony , this group stood shoulder to shoulder across the width of the stage: Kareem Abdul-Jabbar, Bill Russell, Elgin Baylor, Larry Bird, Julius Erving, Isiah Thomas, Dominque Wilkins, Tom Heinsohn, Ray Allen and Harlem Globetrotters CEO Mannie Jackson. Multiple Mount Rushmores worth of basketball royalty, all there in the service of one inductee who averaged 6.7 points and 5.9 rebounds in six professional seasons. And one who died more than 35 years ago, at age 57, in February 1984. Charles Henry (Chuck) Cooper, selected by the Early African-American Pioneers Committee, might possibly have had the most impressive roster of presenters in the history of the Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame. It\u2019s a tradition of the Hall that each new member gets ushered in on his or her big night by one or more already-enshrined legends. Most ask one or two inductees with whom they shared a particular connection. Former Milwaukee All-Star Sidney Moncrief, for example, had former Bucks coach Don Nelson and teammate Bob Lanier at his side. International pick Vlade Divac was presented by Jerry West, the man who brought Divac to the Los Angeles Lakers. WNBA star Teresa Weatherspoon, selected by the Women\u2019s Committee, was accompanied by peers (and rivals) Cynthia Cooper, Sheryl Swoopes and Tina Thompson. And so on. But Cooper, in his posthumous recognition, stirred enough respect from men whose careers famously and statistically dwarfed his because of what he achieved to help make so many of theirs possible. Cooper was the first black player drafted into the NBA, joining the Boston Celtics when they selected him in the second round in April 1950. He is credited with two others in integrating the league in that 1950-51 season. And Cooper was the last of the three to make it to the Springfield shrine, finally joining Nat \u201cSweetwater\u201d Clifton and Earl Lloyd. Clifton, who previously had played for the Globetrotters, is known for becoming the first African-American player to sign an NBA contract, signing a deal with the New York Knicks that offseason. Lloyd, a ninth-round pick in 1950, was actually the first black to play in an NBA game, thanks to Washington opening its schedule before either Boston or New York. None of them came to be as well-known as Jackie Robinson, who broke baseball\u2019s color barrier more than three years earlier and did so as a solo act with the Brooklyn Dodgers. Cooper, Clifton and Lloyd had each other, though that didn\u2019t help much with each playing and traveling with different teams. Each faced the same sort of discrimination as Robinson, from taunts of fans to being refused service at hotels and restaurants. \u201cEven though they were held to a higher standard, they never faltered,\u201d said Chuck Cooper III, accepting on his father\u2019s behalf. That \u2013 and the strides the NBA and basketball overall have taken off that trio\u2019s early steps \u2013 was what stirred the star-studded show of strong, silent support Friday. Presenters don\u2019t make acceptance speeches. At most, they\u2019re there to absorb some teasing if their inductee is fighting initial jitters. But the Who\u2019s Who symbolically backing Cooper didn\u2019t have to say a word. \u201cThat was serious,\u201d Hall of Famer Spencer Haywood said after the ceremony. \u201cThere was something special going on tonight. Something spiritual.\u201d Devout Christians Bobby Jones and Paul Westphal didn\u2019t shy away from talking about and crediting their faith. Weatherspoon was evangelical, too, in a speech that flipped what sounded like a joke about three frogs into an inspirational lesson in perseverance. Moncrief , Divac , Jack Sikma and others spent more time talking about character than crossovers. Sikma and representatives of the Wayland Baptist University women\u2019s teams from 1948-82 had the largest \u2014 or at least most vocal \u2014 fan-and-family contingents. Family members appeared on behalf of player Carl Braun , an early Knicks star also elected posthumously, and coach Bill Fitch , who did not travel due to health. Al Attles, 82, a Warriors\u2019 player, coach and executive for 60 years, attended but taped his speech in advance. So did Dick Barnett , also 82, the former Knicks guard who represented Tennessee A&I\u2019s consecutive NAIA title teams from 1957-59 before the Boston Celtics or UCLA Bruins ever thought of a \u201cthree-peat.\u201d Barnett spoke forcefully about the racial injustices he and his teammates encountered, barred as they were from participating in the NCAA. He talked of opening eyes and changing some minds, too. \u201cIt was teams like Tennessee A&I that made me appreciate the game,\u201d Abdul-Jabbar said in a video. The Class of 2019 slipped in between last year\u2019s group heavy on household names \u2013 notably, Grant Hill, Jason Kidd, Steve Nash and Allen \u2013 and a 2020 crew that is expected to feature icons Kobe Bryant, Tim Duncan and Kevin Garnett. It made for some newfound appreciation Friday for some performers and contributors who might have been missed the first time around or had their talents and achievements dusted off for a global television audience. No one fit that category more than Cooper, who waited longer for Hall recognition than Lloyd (2003) or Clifton (2014). Upon drafting the 6-foot-5 forward from Duquesne, Celtics founder Walter Brown reportedly said, \u201cI don\u2019t give a damn if he\u2019s striped, plaid or polka-dot.\u201d Brown\u2019s decision was credited years later by Lloyd for his team\u2019s willingness to take him seven rounds later. \u201cIf the Celtics did not draft Chuck in the second round, you could not tell me that the Washington Capitols in 1950 were going to make me the first black player to play in this league,\u201d Lloyd once told the Boston Globe. \u201cNo way. The Boston Celtics had a tremendous influence on my acceptance in the NBA.\u201d Cooper\u2019s immediate embrace by coach Red Auerbach, roommate Bob Cousy and teammate Heinsohn aided the integration of other teams that changed their outlooks or felt some pressure to stay competitive. Cousy, for instance, talked about Cooper last year in the book The Last Pass that focused heavily on his relationship with Russell, the NBA\u2019s first black star. The Celtics point guard recalled a night in Raleigh, North Carolina, after a neutral site game in February 1952, when Cooper wasn\u2019t permitted to stay with the team at a segregated hotel. He volunteered to take an overnight train to New York for their next game, and Cousy volunteered to join him. Waiting at the station, they were faced with \u201cwhites only\u201d and \u201ccolored only\u201d restrooms, a first for both men. Cousy suggested they walk to the end of the platform to relieve themselves in the darkness. Standing side by side, they did. \u201cThe Rosa Parks moment, we couldn\u2019t talk about,\u201d Cousy said years later. That helps explain why all those legends were on the stage Friday, standing shoulder to shoulder for Chuck Cooper. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Chuck Cooper and Carl Braun were inducted posthumously, and Bill Fitch was unable to attend for health reasons. Also honored at Springfield\u2019s Symphony Hall were the 1957-59 teams from Tennessee A&I, a historically black school that won three straight NAIA titles a decade before Texas Western fielded the first all-black starting five in the NCAA Tournament. The Hutcherson Flying Queens from Wayland Baptist \u2013 the first school to offer women\u2019s basketball scholarships \u2013 were also inducted. Sponsored by a local aircraft company, they won a record 131 consecutive games in the 1950s. Sikma had a large contingent to support him, and they broke into laughter when the video screens showed a picture of him with a blonde bowl cut shortly after he was drafted in 1977. A seven-time All-Star in nine seasons with the Sonics, he also played five years with the Milwaukee Bucks and remains the only center in league history to lead the league in free throw percentage for a season (.922 in 1987-88). That makes it somewhat less exclusive than the minting of a coin \u2013 which is part of this year\u2019s ceremony as well. The 2019 enshrinement ceremony at Symphony Hall Friday night was accompanied by the unveiling of a 2020 Hall of Fame commemorative coin, part of the U.S. Mint\u2019s official program. Designed to celebrate basketball and its players, the Naismith-themed coin is one of just two commemorative coins produced each year by the U.S. Mint. The curved and, in some versions, colorized coins were to make their debut during the enshrinement ceremony broadcast on NBA TV. Hall of Famers Rick Barry and Tina Thompson, the coin\u2019s two artists who were selected during the design competition, and David Ryder, director of the U.S. Mint, were scheduled to participate. The surcharges for the coins will be $35 for gold, $10 for silver and $5 for clad. The U.S. Mint will produce 50,000 gold coins, 400,000 silver and 750,000 clad versions and make them available for purchase next spring, with revenue going to the Hall to help fund its major renovation as well as ongoing expenses.  The Naismith coin is the result of three years of work and lobbying, passing it through Congress before President Trump signed it into law in December. Ryder said he envisions the coin appealing both to hoops fans and coin enthusiasts, not merely the subset of those who enjoy both. \u201cIf they sell \u2018em all out, it will raise about $9.2 million for the Hall of Fame,\u201d Ryder said. \u201cSports is a huge seller to a lot of our customers. With the marketing pull in sports organizations, it will draw that much more traffic.\u201d The 2019 program commemorating the Apollo 11 moon landing, Ryder said, generated nearly $55 million in sales. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Qi Zhou (13 points and 14 rebounds) powered China with a balanced game, while Guna Ra played all 40 minutes and led Korea with 21 points and 12 rebounds."},{"id":31944,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/hall-fame-presser-jack-sikma-pivot","date":"2019-09-06","title":"Hall of Fame: Jack Sikma's reverse pivot clears lane to induction","content":" SPRINGFIELD, Mass. \u2014 When Jack Sikma officially enters the Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame Friday night, one of his presenters will be Kareem Abdul-Jabbar. Which is a terrific example of game recognizing game. Or in this case, move recognizing move. Just as Abdul-Jabbar ranks as the NBA\u2019s most prolific scorer and arguably its greatest player ever, so does his signature sky hook loom as the league\u2019s most famous individual move. Most unstoppable, too, and for defenders, most deflating. Well, Sikma had a signature move of his own, one that helped elevate him from an NAIA program at Illinois Wesleyan to seven NBA All-Star appearances, a championship with the Seattle SuperSonics in 1979 and now to the brink of his craft\u2019s highest honor. It was the reverse pivot or inside pivot, which were its names when it was an arcane maneuver used by a small number of big men, taught mostly at the sport\u2019s lower levels. Once Sikma learned it in 1974, brought it with him to the NBA in 1977 and helped the Sonics reach The Finals as a rookie and win the championship a year later, though, it swiftly became known as his: The Sikma move. \u201cIt was just an experiment after my freshmen season,\u201d Sikma said Thursday at the Hall, after being introduced at a news conference as one of the Class of 2019\u2019s 12 honorees. Others being inducted this weekend: coach Bill Fitch; NBA stars Bobby Jones, Sidney Moncrief and Paul Westphal; as well as WNBA legend Teresa Weatherspoon. Longtime Warriors player, coach and executive Al Attles was elected as a contributor. NBA center and current Sacramento GM Vlade Divac was chosen by the international committee. Being honored posthumously are: guard Carl Braun, a star in the 1950s; and Chuck Cooper, the first black player drafted by an NBA team (Boston, 1950). Finally, there are two college team entries: the Wayland Baptist women\u2019s teams from 1948-82 and the Tennessee A&I teams from 1957-59. Divergent paths, compelling stories all. Including the one about the slender, blond kid from Kankakee, Illinois, whose offensive game needed a makeover. \u201cMy college coach [Dennie Bridges] and I sat down and he said, \u2018Jack, you\u2019ve just got to be more effective in the post if you want to take the next step.\u2019 I was a good shooter \u2013 I\u2019d learned the game as a wing and grew late, so I was comfortable facing the basket. \u201cHe had a friend who suggested, \u2018Hey, down in southern Illinois there are some coaches who do an inside pivot with their guys to face the hoop. It might create a little space for Jack.\u2019 I was really thin \u2013 I just wanted to get dislodged from the defender.\u201d Basically, Sikma choreographically held a mirror to the post-up moves of traditional centers of the time. Holding the ball with his back to the basket, rather than turning on his pivot foot to the outside and attacking over his shoulder, he would spin to the inside. That motion would set up him a few feet back, facing the hoop, allowing for a simple hop and shot. And then there\u2019s this priceless Hubie Brown interview, in which Sikma teaches the move: \u201cA lot of coaches would pooh-pooh it because you\u2019re catching the ball in one spot and then you\u2019re stepping three feet farther away from the basket,\u201d Sikma said. \u201cThat\u2019s not the concept of big-men play, right? But I\u2019ve got to hand it to Coach. He said, \u2018Jack, I think this is it.\u2019 And I said, \u2018I\u2019m not uncomfortable with the pivot.\u2019\u201d Sikma went from averaging 15.4 points as a freshman to 20.3 as a sophomore, with his shots increasing from 14.5 per game to 17.9. By his senior season, he averaged 27.0 points. As Sikma honed it in the NBA, at 6-foot-11, he would hold the ball above his head with a high release point that gave him the option of flipping up his shot or faking, then powering inside. In 14 seasons, by Sikma\u2019s count, he played against 15 Hall of Fame centers, including Abdul-Jabbar. So he wanted every edge he could get. \u201cYou didn\u2019t know which way he was going to go with it,\u201d said fellow inductee Bobby Jones, a Sikma contemporary known for his defensive prowess. \u201cMost of the time he would go back and shoot that shot, but sometimes he would go forward and draw the contact. I was just sitting there thinking, with all the other [inductees], if I ever blocked his shot. And I don\u2019t think I ever did.\u201d Jones, at 6-foot-9, matched up with him early in Sikma\u2019s career (when Sikma was cast as a power forward for Seattle). Later, Jones had to decide how much help to give the teammate guarding Sikma. \u201cThe only thing I could ever have done was maybe come from behind and get him,\u201d Jones said. \u201cBut he was a pretty good passer too. To ever leave your man that much, there\u2019s a danger there.\u201d Opponents weren\u2019t the only ones made uncomfortable by Sikma\u2019s unusual tactic. \u201cI know I surprised some of the officials because I got called for traveling a few times,\u201d Sikma said. \u201cAnd I said, \u2018Nope, I\u2019m not traveling. I\u2019ve got my foot up in the air, I plant it and then I pivot on it. By stepping out, that creates the space.\u2019 \u201cSo even though it was a long time ago, they had film. They checked it out and they realized it wasn\u2019t a walk. But I got called two or three times doing it.\u201d Sikma laughed, recalling chatty Sonics teammate Fred Brown pleading his case for him to some of the referees. \u201cI\u2019d get called and Fred was in the ref\u2019s face, \u2018That\u2019s his move! That\u2019s his move! It\u2019s not a travel,\u201d Sikma said. \u201cFred had seen it enough in practice and figured it out.\u201d Sikma had another facet to his game with which current NBA fans might be more familiar: he was a protypical \u201cstretch 5.\u201d Said Sidney Moncrief, another 2019 Hall newcomer who played for Milwaukee before and after Sikma was traded there for his final five seasons: \u201cPeople don\u2019t remember this about Jack Sikma, but Don Nelson was the first coach who started emphasizing 3-point shots for big men. He put Jack on the perimeter to take the big men out of the lane so we could make plays.\u201d Not unlike current Bucks center Brook Lopez, Sikma underwent a late-career transformation as a deep threat. In his first 11 seasons, Sikma took 68 3-pointers and made seven (10.3 percent). During his final three seasons \u2013 from age 33 to 35 \u2013 Sikma shot 550 times from behind the arc and made 196 (35.6 percent). Still, it\u2019s the quick inside step about 10 feet from the hoop that puts Sikma in a select subset of Hall of Famers already enshrined and those who will be. Call it the Alcove of Famous Moves. Hakeem Olajuwon\u2019s \u201cDream Shake,\u201d Kevin McHale\u2019s up-and-under, George Gervin\u2019s finger roll, Dominique Wilkins\u2019 double-pump reverse, Allen Iverson\u2019s crossover, Dirk Nowitzki\u2019s one-legged fadeaway and Abdul-Jabbar\u2019s sky hook will be joined, in time, by James Harden\u2019s step-back 3-pointer, Manu Ginobili\u2019s Euro-step, LeBron James\u2019 chase-down block and Steph Curry\u2019s long range pull-up 3. Each became or has become a signature move. But that only matters if the idea works. \u201cThey made it look good, so it was effective,\u201d Sikma said. \u201cIf I tried to do the sky hook, if I tried to do the up-and-under, you\u2019d probably think, \u2018Meh, that\u2019s not such a good move.\u2019 A lot of it has to do with how effective a person is doing it.\u201d The 2019 Enshrinement Ceremony at Springfield\u2019s Symphony Hall will air on NBA TV Friday beginning at 6:30 p.m. ET. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. A member of the Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame, Barkley is one of the most beloved athletes ever to play in Philadelphia. Named one of the 50 Greatest Players in NBA History in 1996, Barkley spent the first eight seasons of his storied career with Philadelphia after the 76ers chose him with the No. 5 overall pick in the 1984 NBA Draft. Over the course of Barkley\u2019s eight years with the 76ers, he tallied 23.3 points, 11.6 rebounds, 3.7 assists and 1.7 steals in 37.3 minutes per game. The \u201cRound Mound of Rebound\u201d shot at least .540 from the field in each season, including a career-best .600 mark in 1989-90. During his tenure in Philadelphia, Barkley was named an All-Star in five straight seasons between 1987-92, as the team reached the playoffs six times, winning 50-plus games three times. He led the league in rebounding in 1986-87, with an average of 14.6 boards per game. One of the most accomplished 76ers off all-time, Barkley ranks in the top-10 in franchise history in offensive rebounds (first), defensive rebounds (first), field-goal percentage (second), total rebounds (third), free throws (fourth), steals (fifth), points (fifth), field goals (sixth) and minutes played (eighth). His .576 field-goal percentage as a 76er is second only to fellow Hall of Famer Wilt Chamberlain (.583). Following his rookie season, Barkley was selected to the 1984-85 All-Rookie First Team, which kicked off a streak of postseason honors lasting his entire Philadelphia career. He was named to the All-NBA Second Team three times and the All-NBA First Team on four occasions. All told for his NBA career, Barkley appeared in 1,073 games (1,012 starts) for Houston, Phoenix and Philadelphia, averaging 22.1 points, 11.7 rebounds, 3.9 assists and 1.5 steals in 36.7 minutes per contest. He was an 11-time All-Star, 11-time All-NBA selection and won the 1992-93 NBA MVP while leading the Suns to the NBA\u2019s best record and an NBA Finals appearance. Barkley, Kareem Abdul-Jabbar, Chamberlain, Karl Malone, Tim Duncan and Kevin Garnett are the only players in NBA history to post at least 20,000 points, 10,000 rebounds and 4,000 assists in their careers. Barkley\u2019s No. 34 was retired by the team in 2001, and this sculpture will now join fellow 76ers legends Billy Cunningham, Wilt Chamberlain, Maurice Cheeks, Julius Erving, Hal Greer and Bobby Jones along 76ers Legends Walk at the Philadelphia 76ers Training Complex."},{"id":32033,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/team-usa-tops-japan-readies-greece","date":"2019-09-05","title":"Team USA faces an MVP-sized challenge to start second round","content":" SHANGHAI \u2014 Once the United States Men\u2019s National Team went up 13-0 against Japan on Thursday, the more interesting development in regard to the Americans\u2019 path to the elimination rounds was taking place about 200 miles away in Nanjing, where Greece and New Zealand were playing for the final spot in the second round of the FIBA World Cup . It was just a five-point game midway through the fourth quarter, but Greece held on for a six-point win, setting up a matchup with the United States in Shenzhen on Saturday ( 8:30 a.m. ET ). That will be one of two games that the Americans play in second-round Group K , the other coming Monday against Brazil. The top two teams from each second round group advance to the quarterfinals, and first round records (and point differentials) carry over to the second round. So the game against Greece and reigning Kia MVP Giannis Antetokounmpo will not be a do-or-die situation for the U.S., which can probably lose one of its two games in Shenzhen and still advance to the knockout rounds. Greece, because it has already lost to Brazil, probably needs to win both of its games. No matter which side has more urgency, its a matchup that we\u2019ve been looking forward to since before this tournament started. It\u2019s the USA vs. the MVP, maybe the first time since prior to 1992 that the U.S. didn\u2019t have the best player on the floor in a game at the Olympics or World Cup. The Americans just don\u2019t see it as something special. * FIBA World Cup recap: Sept. 5 \u201cYou\u2019re excited about a challenge,\u201d Donovan Mitchell said. \u201cEvery matchup is great. But if we start to look at it as a bigger game than what it is, you lose sight of what we\u2019re focusing on. So we\u2019ll just approach it like a regular game and do what we do.\u201d \u201cWe know the MVP\u2019s on that team, of course,\u201d Kemba Walker added. \u201cBut we\u2019re just going to treat it as another game. There can\u2019t be any let up, and the U.S. has to keep getting better each time it plays, because, frankly, it hasn\u2019t been the best team in this tournament to this point. A loss to Greece would put a ton of pressure on the U.S. in its game against Brazil (currently 3-0) on Monday. Their one-point, overtime victory over Turkey surely put a scare in the Americans. And though it\u2019s hard to compare that performance with Thursday\u2019s against an overmatched Japan squad , they clearly played with a little more focus and energy on the final night of the first round. \u201cObviously, you play with a sense of urgency,\u201d Joe Harris said. \u201cOur game the other night against Turkey was a realization that everybody can compete in this tournament. And then you watch their game today with [the Czech Republic] beating them. FIBA is a one-and-done type tournament. It\u2019s similar to March Madness, where there\u2019s upsets all the time. A 33-point halftime margin also allowed the Americans to try out their 2-3 zone defense for most of the second half. They probably won\u2019t ever use it in such a heavy dose again, but getting some reps in on Thursday could give coach Gregg Popovich the confidence to utilize it in a time of need (and to take an opponent out of rhythm) going forward. \u201cTeams do it to us,\u201d Mitchell said, \u201cand I think we got to be able to do it against other teams.\u201d Turkey did it to them on Tuesday, and that was a big reason the Americans blew a 15-point lead and probably should have lost. Serbia finished the game with 24 assists on 27 made field goals. Italy was led by Oklahoma City forward Danilo Gallinari, who scored 26 points with eight rebounds. Italy (2-1) and Serbia both advance to the second round after finishing in the top two spots on Group D. Both teams advance to Group J, which will include the top finishers from Group C (Spain and Puerto Rico). Spain completed an undefeated run through Group C with a 73-65 win over Iran on Wednesday. Toronto Raptors center Marc Gasol led the team with 16 points and six rebounds, while Phoenix Suns guard Ricky Rubio added five points and five assists. Spain now heads to Wuhan, China for Group J, which will include Serbia, Italy and Puerto Rico. Iran finished the group stage with an 0-3 record. Former NBA player Hamed Haddadi tallied 10 points, 15 rebounds and six assists in the loss to Spain. Argentina finished Group B action with a 69-61 win over Russia to improve to 3-0 in the tournament. Facundo Campazzo led the team with 21 points, six rebounds and seven assists, while former NBA veteran Luis Scola added 13 points and eight rebounds in 32 minutes. Argentina advances to Group I, where they will compete against Russia, Poland and Venezuela in Foshan, China. Russia, who had already punched their ticket to the next round before the loss on Wednesday, finishes Group B action with a record of 2-1. The tournament run for host country China ended in disappointment with a 72-59 loss to Venezuela in Beijing. Venezuela was led by Heissler Guillent, who tallied 15 points and eight rebounds in the win. Venezuela advances to Group I, where they will play Poland, Argentina and Russia. Former NBA player Yi Jianlian scored 11 points with eight rebounds in the loss. China finishes the tournament with a record of 1-2. Cote d\u2019Ivoire finished the tournament with an 0-3 record. Charles Abouo was a bright spot for his team and scored 23 points, six rebounds and two assists in the loss to Poland. Puerto Rico punched their ticket to the second round with a 67-64 win over Tunisia. Former NBA player Renaldo Balkman was the leading scorer for Puerto Rico with 14 points and nine rebounds. Puerto Rico advances to Group J, where they will compete against Spain, Serbia and Italy. Tunisia (1-2) is eliminated from the tournament with the loss. This team does not have the superstar bucket-getters of past USA squads. As we saw in the Americans\u2019 overtime escape against Turkey on Tuesday, the margin for error is thin. And to keep winning against tougher competition, the players will need to help each other when it comes to identifying the opposing defense or who\u2019s defending whom on a pick and roll. Just like they do when it comes to identifying the Czech Republic on an unlabeled map of Europe. That has been one of the challenges that U.S. head coach Gregg Popovich has given his players along the journey to the World Cup knockout rounds. At the beginning of some of the team\u2019s meetings, Popovich has selected a player to answer a non-basketball question, a \u201cPop Quiz\u201d if you will. This group of Americans is probably more learned in pick-and-roll coverage than world geography. Fortunately, players, in the face of trying to distinguish the Czech Republic from Slovakia, are allowed to use a lifeline, like on \u201cWho Wants to Be a Millionaire?,\u201d with Popovich in the role of Regis Philbin. Need help? Your teammate is there for you. \u201cBeing willing to ask for help, willing to go to your teammate,\u201d Donovan Mitchell told NBA.com, \u201cthat\u2019s what, really, a lot of this is all about.\u201d It\u2019s not all geography. Stanford alum Brook Lopez aced a Pop Quiz by recalling the periodic symbol for silver (Ag). The key was a mnemonic device that Lopez had memorized years back. \u201cAhh gee, it\u2019s only silver,\u201d he said. \u201cAy you, give me that gold. \u201cI\u2019m amazed at the things I do remember and the things I don\u2019t remember from high school chemistry and high school world cultures.\u201d Harrison Barnes, former AP student , was cited as being the best Pop Quiz contestant. \u201cDerrick White\u2019s eliminated automatically,\u201d Mitchell said. \u201cbecause he\u2019s used to it [playing for Popovich\u2019s San Antonio Spurs]. I would say the best has been Harrison Barnes. We haven\u2019t had too many guys go up yet. But we\u2019ve all chipped in.\u201d \u201cI think the only person who got one right was Harrison,\u201d Jayson Tatum added. \u201cEverybody else had to ask a friend.\u201d One player needed to call on Mitchell\u2019s help to identify China \u2014 the third largest country in the world and the one the players are spending almost three weeks in \u2014 on the map. Mitchell wouldn\u2019t divulge the identity of the teammate who called that lifeline, but said that it was only for confirmation. \u201cThey just didn\u2019t want to be wrong,\u201d he noted. \u201cThey wanted to be sure.\u201d The reward for getting a question right? Popovich transforms from Regis Philbin to Oprah Winfrey. \u201cPop\u2019s handing out cars,\u201d Lopez laughed. \u201cHe\u2019s giving people cars for getting right answers.\u201d Matchbox cars, sadly. The real rewards are intangible. \u201cIt builds camaraderie,\u201d Lopez said. \u201cIt\u2019s good to know that stuff, too.\u201d \u201cThere\u2019s a lot of other stuff going on besides basketball,\u201d USA assistant coach Steve Kerr added. \u201cWhen you\u2019re playing in the World Cup, it takes on a different meaning, playing against teams from all over the world. It\u2019s good for these guys, for all of us, to learn more about the world and learn more about the teams we\u2019re playing. It keeps guys on their toes, but I think they enjoy it too.\u201d Pop Quizzes get the team engaged before the basketball work begins. There\u2019s no wiping the sleep out of your eyes upon entering the room. \u201cHe definitely challenges you mentally,\u201d Barnes said of Popovich. \u201cEvery day, he\u2019s got something different. He\u2019s got a history lesson with a quote from some general during the Civil War. Then he\u2019s got geography. He has a different way of trying to see where you are mentally.\u201d These players are only under Popovich\u2019s watch for six weeks. But even with a condensed timeline between the first day of training camp and a (likely) quarterfinal where the U.S. is facing elimination against a tough opponent, there\u2019s a need to mix things up a little. \u201cYou think back to who your favorite teachers were in your high school days,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cIt\u2019s always the ones that shook things up and made them more interesting, did things that you weren\u2019t expecting, rather than just droned on about the same thing every day. Pop\u2019s just a creative teacher, and I think guys really enjoy that dynamic.\u201d The U.S. players were not discouraged about needing Turkey to miss four straight free throws to win their first-round game on Tuesday. Similarly, Lopez takes a positive view towards the team\u2019s performance in the classroom. \u201cI think we\u2019ve fared pretty well,\u201d he said. \u201cWe\u2019ve missed one or two countries as a team. But our team\u2019s general trivia, capitals, chemistry, and country knowledge is pretty formidable, I think.\u201d \u201cSince the first day that we did it,\u201d Kerr remarked, \u201cI think guys have been preparing a little bit better.\u201d Alas, there is no trivia competition at the World Cup. These guys will only be going head-to-head with other nations on the basketball court. But maybe the bonds built in the face of Pop Quizzes will pay off when the single-elimination rounds begin next week. * * * John Schuhmann is a senior stats analyst for NBA.com. The team that escaped with the win did not look like a team that can win the gold medal in Beijing in less than two weeks. Heck, it didn\u2019t look like a team that will get out of a quarterfinal matchup with Australia, France or Lithuania. Turkey is a good team, a physical squad that executed well against the U.S. defense. But it\u2019s probably not as good as those three teams that the U.S. could face one week from Wednesday. And after just two games, we know that if the Americans reach the quarters, their opponent will be one of those three (unless the Dominican Republic somehow makes it out of the second round against that group). At least one of those three teams won\u2019t even reach the quarterfinals. But if there was any thoughts of the U.S. being in trouble going forward, it wasn\u2019t coming from the players. To them, they got a tough win against a tough opponent. Kemba Walker was asked if he came out of the game, obviously a less-than-stellar team performance, discouraged. \u201cDiscouraged? No,\u201d he replied. \u201cFar from discouraged. We won. No matter how it comes, no matter what. Much respect to Turkey. Those guys played great. It was a battle the whole game. But for us to come out with a win like that, that was incredible. I\u2019m not discouraged at all.\u201d Donovan Mitchell, meanwhile, believes that getting a win in a tough situation like that was a sign of a team that\u2019s mentally strong. \u201cI don\u2019t think we get through a win like this without being as connected and close as we are,\u201d Mitchell said. \u201cWe\u2019ve done a great job of that and have only been together for a month or so. I\u2019m so proud of everybody on the team, man. I know it\u2019s one game. I know we got a lot more to go, but this really builds character and it shows what type of group that we have. Guys were really locked in. Jayson [Tatum] and Kemba and Khris, those three at the end, they really stepped up big.\u201d Tatum had similar thoughts. \u201cWe\u2019re not going to win every game by 30 or 40,\u201d he said. \u201cTurkey\u2019s a great team and they\u2019ve been playing together for a while. This was good for us. It was a tough game, tough battle. A lot of guys gave second, third and fourth efforts, and we learned a lot about ourselves tonight, especially in a tough environment like this. This was a big win. Walker\u2019s thought\u2019s on his team\u2019s zone offense? \u201cOh, we gotta work on that,\u201d he said. \u201cWe gotta work on that.\u201d The U.S. led the game by 15 points (41-26) midway through the second quarter. Up to that point, Turkey was mostly playing man-to-man defense. And after that point, Turkey mostly played a 2-3 zone, with some zone pressure (both 2-2-1 and 1-3-1) to take some time off the clock. Simply put, the U.S. was not very good against the zone. After scoring 41 points (1.37 points per possession) on their first 30 possessions of the game, the Americans scored just 40 points (0.87 per) on their last 46 possessions of regulation. This wasn\u2019t even the toughest Turkey zone that the United States has seen over the years. In the 2010 World Championship gold medal game, Turkey had a zone that sometimes featured five guys 6-foot-8 or taller (with Hedo Turkoglu playing point guard). But this zone still extended out to the 3-point line with size on the wings. The Americans often seemed indecisive against the zone. There was some (ball and player) movement, but not enough. There was some attacking with the dribble, but not enough. There was too much holding onto the ball. \u201cWe definitely need to shore some stuff up,\u201d Harris said. \u201cI think our execution in the halfcourt wasn\u2019t great. We weren\u2019t running stuff to score against the zone. We kind of let them match up against us and take us out of our rhythm a little bit vs. running stuff hard regardless of being zone or man.\u201d \u201cIt\u2019s about being poised,\u201d Mitchell added. \u201cA lot of it just took us by surprise. That\u2019s all it is.\u201d The good news is that they had this experience in Game 2 and now know what it feels like to play against a sizable zone defense. They could certainly work on it in practice (they haven\u2019t had an off-day practice since Friday and won\u2019t be practicing on Wednesday), but practice isn\u2019t necessarily a meaningful simulation. \u201cYou can\u2019t replicate the environment we were just in,\u201d Mitchell said. \u201cYou got to go through it and experience it. There\u2019s so much room for improvement for us, and I\u2019m glad we were able to be a part of that game. And I\u2019m glad we were able to win.\u201d This was a physical game. Just how physical? Afterward, Mitchell got a text from former Utah Jazz teammate Ricky Rubio (who\u2019s playing for Spain in Group C). It read, \u201cWelcome to FIBA.\u201d And it was physical on both ends of the floor. On offense, the U.S. was met with multiple bodies (and arms) when it attacked the paint. On defense, the U.S. guards were flattened by several ball screens from the Turkish bigs. The U.S. attempted nine more free throws than Turkey (32-23), though the Americans were whistled for one more foul (27-26). \u201cWe have to learn how the game is officiated,\u201d Harrison Barnes said. \u201cThere are some plays that we\u2019re kind of used to how the NBA officiates things. The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":32042,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/sidney-moncrief-hall-fame-profile","date":"2019-09-03","title":"Sidney Moncrief's tenacity on both ends separated him","content":" Like a lot of great achievers, Sidney Moncrief\u2019s greatest motivation came from the things he didn\u2019t want to do. Namely, fail. Flop. Mess up. Over 11 NBA seasons, 10 most successfully with the Milwaukee Bucks, Moncrief channeled his fear of failure and glass-half-empty outlook into five All-Star games, 10 selections to all-NBA or all-defensive teams, 11 playoff appearances and now membership in the Class of 2019 for the Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame. People attending or watching the ceremony figure to see Moncrief smile in one night more than he did on the court across his entire career. As a 6-foot-4, 180-pound shooting guard, Moncrief\u2019s all-around approach had him routinely slotted in from about 1980 through 1986 close behind Magic Johnson and Larry Bird among the league\u2019s best players. So lean you could see veins in his temples pulse as he worked, eyes focused like lasers, Moncrief worked relentlessly on both ends of the floor. He attacked the basket on drives and with post-up moves, developed an outside shot on the fly, perpetually moved both himself and the ball, and dared to crash the boards when dinosaurs still ruled the NBA paint. Defensively, Moncrief was even more tenacious . Coltish to the point of looking spindly, he ran run on his toes to catch and pester the men he guarded, knifing and wincing his way through screens if Milwaukee\u2019s innovative coach Don Nelson wasn\u2019t flexing an early version of today\u2019s switch-everything defense. Throughout, the new Hall of Famer stayed serious. As serious as he was working down his chores list each day before his mother, a hotel maid, got home from work. As serious as when he\u2019d hit the open gym at Parkview High in Little Rock, Ark., and follow a workout routine he\u2019d written down while other kids were just worried about \u201cNext!\u201d As serious as when Moncrief showed up to Bucks training camp for the first time and pushed back immediately against a time-honored NBA tradition. \u201cI was holding out so I wasn\u2019t there at the start,\u201d former teammate Marques Johnson said. \u201cBut once I got there, Junior Bridgeman, Quinn Buckner started telling me just how different of a cat he was. One thing they pointed to, they tried to do the rookie hazing thing to get him to carry the basketballs, and Sid refused to do it. His explanation was, he wasn\u2019t a rookie because he\u2019d been playing all his life. So they cut him some slack.\u201d Said Bridgeman, NBA swingman turned restaurant mogul: \u201cSidney considered himself a player, and the NBA \u2013 even though it was better competition \u2013 still was just basketball. I think that made him the kind of player he became. Mentally, if you feel like \u2018this all isn\u2019t new and it\u2019s just a game,\u2019 then you don\u2019t have the nervousness and the rookie jitters.\u201d Affable and modest off the court, Moncrief was and remains analytical about his playing days and the job that got him, finally, to Springfield. At no point, then or now, was it a joke. \u201cMaximum effort,\u201d he said of his best attributes. \u201cA commitment to being a professional, the way you performed, the way you carried out the game plan, the way you interacted with your teammates. When I watch tape of myself now, what really strikes me is how explosive I was and how many different ways I could score the basketball and play defense. Not being one-dimensional was important to me.\u201d Folks noticed. Only five guards (none since 1996) have been named NBA Defensive Player of the Year: Gary Payton (1996), Michael Jordan (1988), Michael Cooper (1987), Alvin Robertson (1986) and Moncrief. Only the Bucks start won it twice, in 1983 and 1984. Those were the first two times it was presented, as if Moncrief\u2019s game demanded the DPOY\u2019s invention. \u201cPeople forget with Nellie \u2013 and Sidney was the key to it \u2013 we were the best defensive team in the league,\u201d said Garry St. Jean, a Bucks assistant in the \u201880s. \u201cWe were one of the first teams to switch. Remember the double-teams we used? Nellie was ahead of his time defensively. He always studied the rules, and then he\u2019d find a way to break \u2018em. Moncrief was the cornerstone to all that.\u201d St. Jean, now a broadcast analyst of Golden State games, remarked in a Sports Illustrated article 34 years ago that an easy way to earn quick cash was to bet on Moncrief\u2019s squads in Milwaukee\u2019s daily scrimmages. \u201cHe never allowed you to have a bad practice,\u201d St. Jean said last week. \u201cHow many guys can you say that about? He was always, always prepared. He was in great physical shape. His mental state of mind. His toughness. His defensive acumen on and off the ball. \u201cHe had the respect of every player on the team. But he also had the opponents\u2019 respect, the other coaches, everybody. He wasn\u2019t a flamboyant player, but everybody from Billy Cunningham to [Maurice] Cheeks to Dennis Johnson and Danny Ainge and all those guys, thought he was amazing.\u201d Said Nelson by phone from his retirement retreat in Maui: \u201cYou just didn\u2019t want him off the floor because he played both ends all the time. He couldn\u2019t do it physically, of course, but you wanted him out there 48 minutes every night.\u201d Two years after Johnson\u2019s first holdout made him a late witness to Moncrief\u2019s game, his second in the fall of 1981 opened his eyes even wider. \u201cWhen you\u2019re holding out, you don\u2019t want your guys to fall on their faces, but you want them to kind of struggle to be around .500 while you\u2019re gone. Y\u2019know, show they miss you a little bit,\u201d said Johnson, a 2019 Naismith Hall finalist. \u201cBut the Bucks started out 7-2 that year. They weren\u2019t helping my leverage one bit, and Sidney Moncrief was the main culprit, having a fantastic start. I think they were 12-6 by the time I came back from that holdout. I was just glad to get back in the fold. Had Moncrief never played a minute in the NBA, he still had achieved hoops immortality for the photograph that graced Sports Illustrated \u2019s Feb. 13, 1978 edition . In the midst of Arkansas\u2019 32-4 season that would reach that spring\u2019s Final Four, Moncrief was captured soaring to the rim, the basketball cocked behind his head for a killer dunk, with a hammer headline of \u201cHigh on the Hogs,\u201d in reference to the Razorbacks\u2019 unofficial nickname. It is regarded as one of the sports weekly\u2019s most memorable covers, with a look on Moncrief\u2019s face of sheer \u2026 ferocity? Nope, try ouch!  \u201cWhen I took off to dunk that ball, I had started having my [left] knee issues,\u201d Moncrief said more than 41 years later. \u201cI remember my knee giving out a little bit when I planted. So if someone looks at the cover, they see my grimace and might think it\u2019s intensity. It\u2019s more pain.\u201d The toughness to play through that, to gut out 11 seasons after doctors predicted he\u2019d break down in seven or less, came early. Moncrief grew up as the second youngest of seven siblings, a brood spread across about 15 years. His father traveled, his mother worked cleaning houses, then hotel rooms, before assisting in class rooms by the time Sid reached high school. His parents divorced when he was in sixth grade \u2013 later, his stepfather worked fixing leaks for the gas company. Everybody, in their time, had chores. \u201cWe just didn\u2019t have people lying around doing nothing. Summertime, whatever, it didn\u2019t matter. You had to be busy,\u201d Moncrief said. That meant mopping floor, sewing his own clothes and ironing in junior high. He and the others tended to a family garden about 20 minutes from home around school and basketball practice. \u201cMostly vegetables,\u201d he said. \u201cAll the things I didn\u2019t want to eat, we grew.\u201d The discipline of that served the children well \u2013 Moncrief is proud of the adults his siblings are \u2013 and taught him what commitment is. The family at one point had moved into public housing in East Little Rock, by age which is where an overdue asphalt court caught Moncrief\u2019s eye at age 11 or so. Till then, he\u2019d been playing football.  \u201cThe rims had the chain \u2018nets.\u2019 That\u2019s when I started being interested in basketball,\u201d he said. \u201cI still hear the sound when people would make shots. \u201cBy seventh grade, we moved again and a neighbor had a goal \u2013 the worst goal you\u2019d ever want to see. \u2026 In eighth grade, I was just OK. But I was a starter and all-city on the ninth-grade team. That\u2019s when I started really developing as a player. I didn\u2019t know that\u2019s what I was doing, but I\u2019d be out there thinking, \u2018You need to make more layups,\u2019 so that\u2019s what I\u2019d do. I\u2019d dribble the ball more, all sorts of things like that, and I got better.\u201d Charles Ripley, the coach at Parkview, kept their gym open late, so Moncrief would travel there when his own school\u2019s, Hall High, was closed. He already had developed an impatience with wasting time, so kept his workouts and scrimmaging on tight schedules. He blossomed as a player at Hall under coach Oliver Elders, and learned more about character too. One morning in the cafeteria, Moncrief made the mistake of not calling him \u201cCoach Elders.\u201d \u201cI called him \u2018O.E.\u2019 I was a sophomore,\u201d Moncrief later recalled. \u201cHe looked at me and said, \u2018Son, you won\u2019t ever play for me being this disrespectful.\u2019\u201d Elders and Moncrief thrived together, nowhere more so than in the classroom when Moncrief hiked his GPA from slightly above 2.0 to a 3.8 as a senior to nail down his scholarship to Arkansas. That\u2019s where he learned defense and detail from coach Eddie Sutton, and where he teamed with Ron Brewer and Marvin Delph to form the Razorbacks\u2019 entertaining and lethal \u201cTriplets,\u201d as NCAA broadcaster Al McGuire dubbed them. Moncrief was named third team all-American as a junior, first team as a senior, and made 60 percent of his field goal attempts over four college seasons. Sutton spent 75 percent of Arkansas practice time drilling defense, which served his top NBA prospect \u2013 Moncrief is the first Razorbacks player inducted into the Hall of Fame and still ranks as the highest drafted \u2013 well as a pro. Even concerns about his left knee couldn\u2019t scuttle enthusiasm for Moncrief among the league\u2019s scouts. No, that was left to Nelson. The 1979 Draft was all about Magic Johnson, with no clear order for those who would be chosen after him. The Detroit Pistons were desperate to move up to snag Michigan State\u2019s second star, Greg Kelser \u2013 the Spartans had just beaten Larry Bird\u2019s Indiana State team for the NCAA title \u2013 and approached Milwaukee about a deal. \u201cThey gave me a million dollars to move,\u201d Nelson said, chuckling anew about the maneuver. \u201cWe had lost exactly $1 million with the team that year. So with one little move, I could put us in the black. [Note: Several reliable league sources recall the Pistons paying $50,000 rather than $1 million to flip picks, still a considerable sum in 1979.] \u201cTo do the deal, they had to tell me who they were going to take. I figured I could get Sid after that.\u201d Much has been made about Chicago losing the coin flip that year with the Lakers, which cost the Bulls Johnson but helped keep them scroungy enough to get Michael Jordan five years later. In an alternate 1979 reality, though, the Bulls could have opted for Moncrief at No. 2 instead of UCLA forward David Greenwood \u2013 and probably improved enough to miss out on Jordan that way, too. Brian Winters was a bearded, sharpshooting off guard, born and raised in New York, schooled in South Carolina and heading into his sixth NBA season. He\u2019d been an All-Star twice in his first four years with the Bucks. So it wasn\u2019t as if Milwaukee was drafting for need in 1979. \u201cBefore the Draft, Nellie called me and said, \u2018Y\u2019know, Brian, we\u2019re going to draft a guard. He plays your position.\u2019\u201d said Winters, 67, who scouts for the Indiana Pacers. \u201cAll I said to Nellie was, \u2018Is he good? Then draft him. Because I want to play with good players.\u2019 \u201cFairly quiet personality, not overt in his manner, but he fit right in. No problem with me. I felt we got along great. We played well together, competed against each other in practice every day. Never had a problem with it.\u201d Nelson knew where it was headed, but used Moncrief off the bench in 1979-80, even as the Bucks\u2019 victory total jumped from 38 to 49. It was their first of seven straight division titles and 10 consecutive playoff appearances during Moncrief\u2019s time in Milwaukee. \u201cYour teammates make you,\u201d said Moncrief, who will turn 62 in a few weeks. \u201cAnd not just on the court. Junior Bridgeman and Harvey Catchings were my mentors when I came in. They used to show me how to dress, how to work out, how you should approach the game, how to be a professional. They kept me safe on the road. \u201cThose early years in the NBA, if you don\u2019t have a stable person you can be around, it can get a little rocky. I had that with Milwaukee, guys who could keep me focused.\u201d From 1980 to 1984, Moncrief and Marques Johnson were as potent and talented a tandem of shooting guard and small forward as the NBA had. That changed when Nelson traded Johnson, Bridgeman and Catchings to the Clippers in September 1984, replenishing his roster with Terry Cummings, Ricky Pierce and Craig Hodges. \u201cIt broke my heart,\u201d Moncrief says. But success on the court continued, and these days they all choose to remember the good times. \u201cSidney was the first guy I played with who was an ardent, consistent weight lifter,\u201d said Johnson, now a Bucks TV analyst. \u201cHe convinced the Bucks to put a bench press into one of these little musty side rooms in the MECCA, and he\u2019d go in there for 15, 20 minutes. One day I asked him what was going on, and he showed me. So I started lifting with him. That was something that was taboo for me at the time, but he said, \u2018As long as we get up shots afterward, we\u2019ll be fine. We\u2019ll get our touch back.\u2019\u2019\u2019 Defensively, Johnson is convinced there were three or four Bucks victories that were clinched when Moncrief came up with steals to thwart any opposing heroics. \u201cHe was so good at coming up big defensively in big moments,\u201d Johnson said. \u201cI always say Michael Jordan is the greatest two-way player I\u2019ve ever seen. Sidney Moncrief is second.\u201d Moncrief\u2019s position was loaded in the \u201880s with dangerous scorers and fine athletes: Dennis Johnson, Darrell Griffith, Andrew Toney, Otis Birdsong, David Thompson, Walter Davis, World B. Free, Rolando Blackman, George Gervin, Clyde Drexler and Jordan. His head-to-heads with Jordan started well enough, Milwaukee winning five of the first eight meetings and a 3-1 first-round playoff series at the end of Jordan\u2019s rookie season. Then it turned: Jordan\u2019s team went 12-2 over their final 14 clashes. In the 22 regular-season games overall, Jordan averaged 31.6 points to Moncrief\u2019s 14.6. Moncrief\u2019s effective strategy on defense \u2013 take away strengths, nudge them toward weaker moves \u2013 didn\u2019t pay off the same against the Bulls star. Their careers weren\u2019t in sync either, with Moncrief\u2019s knobby knees and other body parts limiting his availability and effectiveness starting in 1986-87, with Jordan\u2019s star fully ascending. He played the equivalent of two seasons over his final three in Milwaukee, averaging 11.6 points and 25.6 minutes. By the end of 1988-89, at age 31, Moncrief called it quits. A year away from the game did wonders for his physical well-being, so he put out feelers in the summer of 1990 and found a taker in Atlanta for a part-time reserve and resident \u201cold head.\u201d Now he calls that season, with Dominique Wilkins, Moses Malone, Doc Rivers and some raw young players, his \u201cmost rewarding year in basketball.\u201d After being a high pick and an All-Star, Moncrief welcomed a smaller role, including winning a roster spot and earning a non-guaranteed contract by averaging 4.7 points in 15.2 minutes. \u201cIt was all about basketball, and I enjoyed it,\u201d he said of his final season. Moncrief left Milwaukee as arguably the second greatest player in franchise history behind Kareem Abdul-Jabbar. And as disappointing as it was that his teams, despite winning nearly 64 percent of their games in his Milwaukee run, never broke through to the Finals, getting eliminated year after year by Boston or Philadelphia makes sense, in hindsight. The Bucks got bounced from the conference finals three times in four years (Sixers in \u201983, Celtics in \u201984 and 86). \u201cWe were good. We got everything out of those teams that we could to do as well as we did,\u201d Moncrief said. \u201cWe were just not the better team during that era.\u201d Conventional wisdom says that those Milwaukee teams, as a result, are underappreciated. Nelson, for instance, ranks the 1983 and 1984 squads as the best he ever coached. But the old Hall of Famer doesn\u2019t agree that the ring not won sticks to individual players.  \u201cYou don\u2019t have to win championships necessarily to have the respect of the Hall,\u201d Nelson said. \u201cI\u2019m a good example. I\u2019ve never won a championship as a coach, but I went into the Hall of Fame as a coach. It took me a while to get in, but that\u2019s fine.\u201d A common question for yesterday\u2019s legends is, how would his game translate to today\u2019s game? With Moncrief, as with so many of the era, it would be unfair to focus on his 3-point field goal percentage (.284). The weapon got to the NBA the same season he did and didn\u2019t merit practice time or game-planning. \u201cIf Sidney had worked on it the way they do know, he\u2019d have been a 36, 37, 38 percent shooter, because of how tough he was mentally,\u201d Marques Johnson said. A current comp, in demeanor if not in level of accomplishment? Johnson called Bucks guard George Hill a \u201cpoor man\u2019s Sid.\u201d \u201cHe\u2019s nowhere near that superstar status, but he\u2019s a guy you\u2019d love playing with. A guy who plays at both ends, knocks down big shots. The main thing that reminds me of Sidney is George\u2019s stoic attitude. He just competes.\u201d Johnson added: \u201cHe was first-class athlete who jumped out of the gym and dunked on Robert Parish and 7-footers on a regular basis. Who led us in rebounding a couple of years. And he worked on his outside shooting so much, he became a better than average shooter. So he\u2019d translate really well to today\u2019s game. He\u2019d be about a $40 million a year player.\u201d Nelson recalled Moncrief playing big with a 35-inch vertical leap, but also his knack for playing small. \u201cHe had a way to make himself thin, to get through seams,\u201d the coach said. \u201cThere was a technique that he used that he taught to other guys on our team. He just had a way to physically force his way in and get so small, and not get picked off by the screens.\u201d As for Moncrief\u2019s demeanor, he never broke character when the ball went up. Yet teammates saw a side of Moncrief the public did not. \u201cLike in all of us, there are two different people,\u201d Bridgeman said. \u201cThe basketball person is exactly like you saw. He was serious when he played, when he was working out, before the games. The game \u2013 like all great players \u2013 was what was important to him. \u201cBut away from the game, it was a different Sidney. We\u2019d laugh. He went to the movies a lot. He told jokes. It was probably different from what people thought.\u201d Moncrief is more relaxed and outgoing these days as a businessman and entrepreneur, his latest incarnation after a dozen different post-playing pursuits. He coached for a few seasons, including with Nelson as a defensive guru in Dallas and Golden State and with the Bucks under Scott Skiles. He has invested in real estate, owned a Buick dealership, served on corporate boards and was thought to have a future in Arkansas politics. Currently, he and his wife of four years, Takisha, run Moncrief One Team, a corporate coaching and motivational program. He has written six books to that end, and resides in the Dallas area, where all but one of his four grown sons (Jon, Brett, Jason, Jeffrey) live. \u201cI\u2019m really introverted by nature,\u201d Moncrief said. \u201cBut my overall life experiences, success and failures, has certainly equipped me to give certain insight to schools and individuals. It\u2019s so natural for me to do what I\u2019m doing. And I love everything about my life right now. If someone can say that, that\u2019s a good thing.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. You can e-mail him here , find his archive here and follow him on Twitter . Japan\u2019s 89-76 loss to the Czech Republic on Tuesday meant Hachimura and his team will likely finish no better than 17th at this 32-team World Cup, but he\u2019s still seeing plenty of upside to his summer. \u201cIt\u2019s been crazy,\u201d Hachimura said. \u201cI got drafted, went to D.C. and we had like a minicamp, then Summer League and now the World Cup. It\u2019s been a crazy summer but I\u2019ve been having fun with this experience, this process. I\u2019ve played a lot of games this summer, everywhere. I don\u2019t know how much I grew, but I have more confidence.\u201d Japan might be winless in its two World Cup games \u2014 and has a matchup against the U.S. looming Thursday \u2014 but the stage has not seemed too big for Hachimura. The 6-foot-9 forward has averaged 18 points so far on 50 percent shooting, while adding 6.5 rebounds, 3 assists and 1.5 steals per game. Wizards general manager Tommy Sheppard even made the trip to Asia to watch Hachimura in person, going to Japan first for the pre-tourney games and then following the team to China. \u201cHe\u2019s going to be a really nice player in the NBA,\u201d Sheppard said. \u201cHe\u2019s got a lot \u2014 a lot \u2014 of upside. He\u2019s got a lot of learning to do, but he\u2019s got excellent skill and excellent will. \u2026 It just takes time, and we have to develop that.\u201d Hachimura\u2019s talent was on obvious display in Las Vegas at Summer League, where he averaged 19.3 points on 50 percent shooting in three games. The Tokyo Olympics are less than a year away, and the NBA \u2014 in a move that should build Olympic buzz \u2014 is sending Toronto and Houston to Japan for a pair of preseason games in October. Those will be the first NBA games in Japan since 2003. And it won\u2019t take much for Hachimura, the West Coast Conference\u2019s player of the year last season at Gonzaga, to become the best Japan-born player in NBA history. He\u2019s the first Japanese player to be a first-round pick and the only two players from the country to appear in an NBA game are Yuta Tabuse in 2004-05 and Hachimura\u2019s World Cup teammate Yuta Watanabe last season. The U.S. trailed by two in the final seconds of regulation and by one in the final seconds of overtime. But it survived thanks to a pair of Jayson Tatum free throws to send the game to overtime and a pair of Khris Middleton free throws for the win. The U.S. opened up a 15-point lead midway through the second quarter, but Turkey responded with a 12-0 run to get back in the game. It stayed close and was tied midway through the fourth. Turkey had multiple chances to take the lead and finally did so with an Ersan Ilyasova tip-in over Joe Harris with 12.3 seconds to go. After Middleton missed an open 3 from the right wing, Donovan Mitchell got his hand on the rebound. Kemba Walker saved the ball back to Mitchell, who found Tatum at the top of the key. He pump-faked one defender out of the play and was fouled by Cedi Osman on a pull-up 3 just before the buzzer. The referees reviewed the play and gave Tatum three free throws with 0.1 seconds left on the clock. He made the first, missed the second, and made the third to give the U.S. a chance in overtime. Down one with 14.6 seconds left in OT, the U.S. inbounded the ball to Myles Turner, who couldn\u2019t find an open man and was stripped. Joe Harris then committed an unsportsmanlike foul (not going for the ball), giving Turkey two free throws and the ball. That could have shut the door, but Dogus Balbay and Osman proceeded to go 0-for-4 at the line. Tatum rebounded the final miss in traffic, drove down the floor and found Middleton cutting down the left side of the lane. He was fouled by Turkey\u2019s Bugrahan Tuncer and converted both free throws with 2.1 seconds left. During the fast break, Tatum sprained his left ankle. He will be re-evaluated on Wednesday. Turkey advanced the ball after Middleton\u2019s made free throws, but Ilyasova missed a step-back three for the win. The Americans struggled to find good shots against Turkey\u2019s 2-3 zone, scoring just 40 points on their final 46 possessions of regulation. They shot just 35 percent from the field and had twice as many turnovers (14) as they had in their win over the Czech Republic on Sunday. Middleton led the U.S. with 15 points. Kemba Walker added 14, including five straight in overtime that erased a five-point Turkey lead. Ilyasova led Turkey with 23 points and 14 rebounds. With the win, the U.S. has qualified for the second round of the World Cup. The winner of the game between Turkey and the Czech Republic on Thursday will be the second team to advance out of Group E. The Americans will close out Group E play with a game against Japan on Thursday. While we closely track the U.S. Men\u2019s National Team, here\u2019s a quick look at all the action from FIBA World Cup play on Sept. 2. * * * Serbia showed the world on Monday why they\u2019re among the favorites to win the 2019 FIBA World Cup. The team shot 75 percent from the field and held Philippines to 37.3 percent in a 126-67 blowout win. Sacramento Kings forwards Nemanja Bjelica and Bogdan Bogdanovic led Serbia with 20 and 17 points, respectively, while Denver Nuggets star Nikola Jokic added 11 points, seven rebounds and seven assists in just 18 minutes of action. The team is now 2-0 in group play after defeating Angola 105-59 on Saturday. Philippines are now 0-2 in the group stage. Former NBA player Luis Scola has led Argentina for nearly two decades and he made history during his team\u2019s 94-81 win over Nigeria on Monday. The 39-year-old forward \u2014 who is taking part in his fifth World Cup \u2014 finished with a team-high 23 points to move into second place for most points all-time in FIBA World Cup history. Scola still trails Brazilian star Oscar Schmidt by nearly 300 points, but Scola isn\u2019t worried about passing the legend. \u201cIt\u2019s impossible. A small part of me \u2013 even if I had the chance to take number one from Oscar \u2013 a part of me doesn\u2019t want to do it. I just want him up there, he\u2019s my big hero. A small part of me would be like, ah, nah, let\u2019s just let him be,\u201d Scola said. Minnesota Timberwolves guard Josh Okogie led Nigeria with 18 points and five assists in the loss. Argentina is now 2-0 in the group stage, while Nigeria is 0-2. Poland outlasted China to win in overtime 79-76 in front of a raucous crowd in Beijing on Monday. Mateusz Ponitka (25 points) and A.J. Slaughter (22) were the leading scorers for Poland during the back-and-forth thriller that saw 13 lead changes. Former NBA player Yi Jianlian led China with 24 points and eight rebounds in 30 minutes. Poland rises to 2-0, while China drops to 1-1 in the group stage. Veteran leaders Marc Gasol and Ricky Rubio powered Spain to a hard-fought 73-63 win over Puerto Rico to improve to 2-0 in the group stage. Gasol did it all (19 points, seven rebounds, two assists, two steals, two blocks), while Rubio added 17 points, eight rebounds and four assists. Spain advances to the second round with the win as they are guaranteed to finish in the top two in Group C. The United States Men\u2019s National Team\u2019s performance in its 88-67 win over the Czech Republic on Sunday wasn\u2019t spectacular. But it was solid, and the U.S. showed that it could work through some early offensive struggles and clamp down defensively when it needed to. Myles Turner summed it up: \u201cWe still got a lot of work to do. I think it was a good first game. We got the jitters out. Everybody was jacked up, amped to play. But on both sides of the ball, we still got a lot of film to analyze.\u201d The Czechs don\u2019t have the same level of talent as the toughest opponents the Americans will potentially face in the knockout rounds. But the offensive elements \u2014 pick-and-roll play, ball movement, back-door cuts \u2014 that will challenge the U.S. defense were there. So holding the opponent to just 67 points on 75 possessions (0.89 per) is a good sign. The Czech Republic shot just 41 percent from the field. They were 8-for-18 (44 percent) from 3-point range, but the 18 attempts is a indication that the U.S. defense was properly aggressive on the perimeter. The Czechs had a moment. It was a 9-0 run that put them up 11-7 midway through the first quarter. They had a couple of nice buckets off cuts in the paint, but the bigger issues for the U.S. were on the other end of the floor, where they went six straight possessions without a score. Turner missed a contested spin move in the post, Jaylen Brown stepped out of bounds on the break, Donovan Mitchell missed a step-back jumper, Khris Middleton missed a catch-and-shoot three, Mitchell missed a pull-up three, and Turner missed a pick-and-pop, foul-line jumper. The only look inside in that sequence was Turner\u2019s post-up. And as great as the Pacers\u2019 center has been for this team through six games, Turner in the post shouldn\u2019t be the only source of inside offense. \u201cI thought we were rushing a lot offensively, just playing really fast,\u201d Kemba Walker said about the early offensive struggles. \u201cMaybe the adrenaline was just pumping so much, the excitement of being here and it being our first game. I thought when we slowed down and played with our pace, that\u2019s when things went right.\u201d Turner\u2019s impact on the other end of the floor made up for his 2-for-7 shooting performance. He blocked a couple of shots early, and the U.S. ended the 9-0 Czech run with his pick-and-roll defense. He first forced Tomas Satoransky to pick up the ball on a drive, and then recovered back to the roll man, so that the airborne Sato had nowhere to go with the ball. The errant pass led to a Mitchell drive in transition. Harrison Barnes and Joe Harris got a couple of more buckets inside and the Americans completed a 10-0 run with another fast break (Kemba Walker feeding Derrick White for a dunk) off another live-ball turnover. With a clogged paint sometimes making it tough to generate offense, those live-ball turnovers are gold. Another one led to a Tatum three that capped a 10-2 run to start the second quarter. That was when the U.S. really took control. \u201cWe have to be a team that\u2019s going to pressure those guys on defense and play in transition,\u201d Walker said afterward. \u201cThat\u2019s who we want to be.\u201d After scoring just 17 points on 20 possessions (0.85 per) in the first quarter, the U.S. scored 71 points on 57 possessions (1.25 per) thereafter. There was another six-possession, scoreless stretch early in the third quarter, though. They can survive two of those against the Czech Republic, but maybe not against the better European teams. The Czech Republic tried early and often to post up their bigger guys (mostly Satoransky) on Walker. But Walker held his own. In fact, the Czechs scored just four points on the seven possessions in which they posted against Walker in the first half. He forced one turnover and Turner forced another when he helped inside. Afterward, the 6-1 Walker was asked if he wants his teammates to come with a double-team when he\u2019s being posted up. \u201cNo, I\u2019m good,\u201d he said. \u201cI tell \u2019em let me play. I\u2019m good down there. I can play some post defense. That\u2019s on them [if they double].\u201d Turkey will have some more big guards to defend on Tuesday. Serbia, meanwhile, doesn\u2019t have anybody shorter than 6-5 on their roster. After getting only 10 3-point attempts off in more than 95 exhibition minutes, Harris got three shots from deep in less than 20 minutes on Sunday. And he knocked the first two down. Whether he\u2019s shooting or not, his spacing is critical, but it\u2019s still good to get the NBA\u2019s leading 3-point shooter some 3-point shots. \u201cThe ball was moving a little bit more today,\u201d Harris said. \u201cThe last few games, we\u2019ve kind of been tinkering with stuff, here and there. Today, we kind of just got out, played aggressively, and tried to play in attack-mode from the get-go.\u201d Harris hasn\u2019t let his shooting define him, though. In almost every game so far, he\u2019s had a couple of big defensive rebounds in traffic. He hit the floor for one loose ball on Sunday. \u201cYou\u2019re playing in really quick minute bursts,\u201d Harris said. \u201cYou\u2019re not leaving anything in the tank. Everybody\u2019s playing very aggressive. You\u2019re doing what you can to help the team. And for me, I\u2019m out there trying to space the floor, knock down shots, and do the little stuff, get the 50-50 balls, crash the offensive glass, and crash the defensive glass.\u201d Speaking of those quick stints on the floor, Harris noted that U.S. head coach Gregg Popovich isn\u2019t exactly forthcoming with how the rotation is going to play out. Players know what their roles are when they\u2019re on the floor, they just don\u2019t know when they\u2019ll be on the floor. \u201cNobody really knows where their playing time is at,\u201d Harris said. \u201cWe just know that when you\u2019re in there, you\u2019re busting your ass.\u201d They won\u2019t be busting their ass on Monday, though. Popovich has decided not to practice on Labor Day, and the team will just hold a shootaround Tuesday morning in preparation for what should be their toughest game in Group E. They face Turkey Tuesday night (8:30 a.m. ET). Every team has played one game, and there weren\u2019t any real surprises over the weekend, though Germany hanging with France in a 78-74 defeat on Sunday is noteworthy in regard to the future of both teams. * * * John Schuhmann is a senior stats analyst for NBA.com. Guy Landry Edi led C\u00f4te d\u2019Ivoire with 10 points."},{"id":32062,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/record-field-set-2019-fiba-world-cup","date":"2019-08-31","title":"Record field set for 2019 FIBA World Cup","content":" SHANGHAI (AP) \u2014 The biggest basketball World Cup is about to begin. Many of the world\u2019s top players \u2014 and a couple of the world\u2019s top national teams \u2014 are not in China for the FIBA World Cup, a 32-team extravaganza that begins Saturday. At stake over the next 16 days: The world championship, along with seven of the 11 remaining available berths in next summer\u2019s Tokyo Olympics. And several teams figure they can be the one to thwart the United States\u2019 bid for an unprecedented third straight crown. \u201cWe\u2019re here to go for gold,\u201d said Sasha Djordjevic, the coach of Serbia \u2014 a team that some consider the tournament\u2019s gold-medal favorite. \u201cEvery game that we play will be the biggest game for us.\u201d The first eight games of the tournament are Saturday, and things will move quite rapidly. The eight-game-a-day pace continues through Sept. 9, with quarterfinal games on Sept. 10 and 11, semifinals on Sept. 13 with the event capped by the gold- and bronze-medal games in Beijing on Sept. 15. All told, 92 games will be played in eight cities. \u201cWe have nothing to lose,\u201d said Japan guard Yuta Watanabe, whose team will face the U.S. in the group stage. FIBA changed much about the tournament for this edition. The event was moved back a year; the last World Cup was in 2014, and it was bumped to 2019 this time around to avoid going against the FIFA World Cup for men\u2019s soccer in the same years. The field was expanded from 24 to 32 and qualifying rules were vastly altered largely to keep NBA and other pro-league players from helping their countries reach the event. For some nations, that became a huge problem. European champion Slovenia, the world\u2019s seventh-ranked team, is not in the World Cup. Same goes for world No. 9-ranked Croatia, which lost eight of its 12 qualifying games. Yet for other nations, the changes sparked opportunity \u2014 Nigeria, Venezuela, Italy and Japan all qualified for the first time since 2006, and Poland made the field for the first time since 1967. \u201cThe World Cup is an unbelievable competition,\u201d said Canada coach Nick Nurse, who doubles as coach of the NBA champion Toronto Raptors. \u201cGreat teams and coaches and scouting and work and preparation that will make anyone better for going through that. So I\u2019m extremely honored and excited and humbled to be here.\u201d Most of the top Americans aren\u2019t in the World Cup, a few because of injuries, other candidates cited schedule concerns. Of the 35 leading scorers from this past NBA season who would have been eligible to play for the U.S. team, only two \u2014 Kemba Walker of the Boston Celtics and Donovan Mitchell of the Utah Jazz \u2013 are wearing the red, white and blue in China. \u201cI\u2019m more concerned with who is here than who isn\u2019t,\u201d U.S. coach Gregg Popovich said. For the international teams, though, there\u2019s minimal concern about big-name absences. Greece is led by NBA MVP Giannis Antetokounmpo of the Milwaukee Bucks, Serbia is led by All-NBA center Nikola Jokic of the Denver Nuggets and France features third-team All-NBA center Rudy Gobert. And the host Chinese are hoping to make a splash as well. \u201cIt\u2019s a rare opportunity for all of us,\u201d former NBA player Yi Jianlian, now the Chinese captain, said in Beijing earlier this week. \u201cYou can feel the special duty when you see the national flag on your chest.\u201d Some other things to know as the World Cup begins: FORMAT Teams were drawn into eight groups of four for the opening round, with the two top teams from each group making the round of 16 and the others going into classification games to determine 17th through 32nd place. Games are four 10-minute quarters, so regulation is eight minutes shorter than an NBA game. The 3-point line is a bit closer than in the NBA, especially from the top of the arc \u2013 that\u2019s 22 feet, 2 inches in FIBA play, or 19 inches closer than the NBA stripe. The top two teams from the Americas, top two from Europe and top regional finisher form Asia, Africa and Oceania will clinch spots to join Japan in the 2020 Olympics; the last four spots in the 12-team field for the Tokyo Games will come down to qualifying next July. BEST GROUP The so-called \u201cgroup of death\u201d would be Group H, with Australia (which just beat the U.S. in a friendly to snap a 78-game winning streak by the Americans), Lithuania and Canada. All three of those teams are likely good enough to advance, yet only two will get to the round of 16 with a shot at the quarterfinals. Senegal rounds out that group. U.S. RECORD The Americans have won 19 consecutive World Cup (formerly known as the world championship) games, and are 14-0 in games in China when using a roster composed of NBA players (8-0 in the 2008 Olympics and 6-0 in other games there in 2006 and 2008). Mason Plumlee is the only player on this year\u2019s U.S. World Cup team that was on the gold-medal-winning roster at this event in 2014. REFRESHER COURSE Seven of the eight quarterfinalists from the 2014 World Cup are in this year\u2019s field. The U.S. beat Lithuania and Serbia beat France in the semifinals, and the Americans rolled to a 129-92 win in the gold-medal game behind 26 points from Kyrie Irving. France rallied in the fourth quarter to beat Lithuania for bronze 95-93. GLOBAL GAME FIBA, basketball\u2019s global organizing body, says the tournament will be televised in at least 176 countries and territories."},{"id":32031,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/lakers-demarcus-cousins-arrest-warrant","date":"2019-08-30","title":"Arrest warrant issued for Lakers center DeMarcus Cousins","content":" MOBILE, Ala.
